I think a very important part to think about with comedy in more serious settings are two things. One, is it balanced enough with the primary tone the show/movie is trying to set. And secondly, would it be something you would laugh at if you were involved. Like the scene with the thaddeus and the music guy and his traps. It's funny to us because we're watching it from an outside perspective. But if we were to be in that situation ourselves we wouldn't be laughing. So, I think being able to do that, while mixing the tone and funny well enough make it work.

I‚Äôm a little late to the party with season 2 right around the corner, but I‚Äôll chime in.  Most of my experience with _Fallout_ comes from playing New Vegas, though I‚Äôve played a good amount of 3 and 4, too.  I enjoyed the show. It was well-made in just about every way. Good writing, good cinematography, etc.  The effects were a little over-the-top and the tone was a bit lighthearted for my tastes (I prefer darker, grittier settings like say, _The Book of Eli_ ), but that‚Äôs just personal preference. There‚Äôs nothing wrong with that at all, and it still feels like _Fallout_ .  I do care about the lore and the show contradicts/retcons what‚Äôs established, but in ways that I can sweep under the rug for the most part.  For example (spoilers in this paragraph), while it was awesome to see Robert House, the fact that he as well as Frederick Sinclair were at the council where the decision to drop the bomb was made implies that they were in on it. That‚Äôs not necessarily an issue, but in New Vegas, it is NOT implied that they were in on the Great War at all. Quite the opposite. You can talk to Mr. House about this in the game and he pretty much says that he couldn‚Äôt save the world, but he could save Vegas, and set out to work immediately back in 2065. He wasn‚Äôt able to prepare everything in time and as a result, almost died. It‚Äôs also odd how some of the people in Vault 32 like Hank are perfectly ambulatory despite being 200 years old, when Mr. House is not, being confined to his life-support chamber, considering he was a ‚Äúbillionaire 30 times over‚Äù by the time he was 30 years old. If they along with all the people who were cryo-frozen can be thawed out without any issues, why can‚Äôt House? He was in a coma for most of those 200 years anyway, and he even says that it was ‚Äúvery costly‚Äù to survive that long. It is NEVER implied that House was conspiring with Vault-Tec to instigate the nuclear war.  The only connection that House has with Vault-Tec is that the Pip-Boys and computer terminals were created by RobCo, House‚Äôs multi-billion-dollar company. While this COULD explain things, again, the implication that House was actually conspiring with Vault-Tec isn‚Äôt found anywhere in New Vegas.  And Sinclair (spoilers here too) had a couple issues too, though these are more nitpicky. First, his only appearance is at the Sierra Madre Casino. You can find his skeleton there right outside the casino vault, and you can find a mosaic of him in the casino itself; he is not overweight or in his 60‚Äôs like the Sinclair you see in the show. Furthermore, you can find bits of lore all over the Sierra Madre, which all implies that the whole casino was built to protect Vera Keyes from a potential nuclear war and its aftermath, though advertised as a place where anyone could ‚Äúbegin again.‚Äù It didn‚Äôt quite work out that way and you can find out what happened by playing the _Dead Money_ DLC for New Vegas, but it‚Äôs NEVER implied that Sinclair was conspiring with Vault-Tec to instigate the nuclear war.  Furthermore, (spoilers in this paragraph), in the show, Sinclair represents Big MT, which doesn‚Äôt make any sense. Big MT is run by the Think Tank plus Dr. Mobius, whom you can meet in the _Old World Blues_ DLC for New Vegas. In the bits of lore you can find in Big MT as well as the Sierra Madre, it‚Äôs shown that Sinclair signed a deal with Big MT to have holograms (for security and services) and vending machines that turn Sierra Madre casino chips into various useful things like food, booze, medicine, and ammo at the casino in exchange for allowing Big MT to use the casino for their own experiments. Behind Sinclair‚Äôs back in this contract was the Cloud, which Big MT unleashed upon the casino and it had lethal effects on people. This is why you see the Ghost People and the Cloud in the Sierra Madre when you visit it; they‚Äôre the construction workers who got sealed up inside their hazmat suits because the Cloud corroded the clamps. It‚Äôs also heavily implied in the lore you find at the Sierra Madre that Sinclair was going through some serious financial hardships and hired Mr. Yesterday to cut as many corners as he could when working on the villa outside the casino while the casino itself was as perfect as money could buy. Sinclair does NOT represent Big MT.  Also, Repconn is represented at the council, which also doesn‚Äôt make a lot of sense because Repconn, against the wishes of its original owner, Leonard Steeple, was purchased by Mr. House in 2075. It was sold in a similar fashion as when Oscorp was almost sold to Quest Aerospace in the first Sam Raimi _Spider-Man_ movie; Steeple was very clear that he wanted Repconn to stay independent, but the offer from Mr. House was too tender for the shareholders to ignore, not to mention the corporate espionage. You can visit the Repconn HQ in New Vegas and find bits of lore about it, and Arcade Gannon will tell you some information about it too, if he comes with you.  I‚Äôll give the show some credit though: after Steeple stepped down, his position was taken over by Julia Masters, and she represents Repconn in the TV show. THAT is a great attention to detail.  The last nitpick I have (though this was clarified by Todd Howard in an interview) is that (again spoilers) it‚Äôs implied that Shady Sands, the capital of the NCR, was destroyed in 2277, four years before the events of New Vegas, which doesn‚Äôt make any sense. Todd clarifies that the detonation actually happens AFTER the events of New Vegas, so it‚Äôs probably just an oversight. Still, it does bother me a little bit that Shady Sands was nuked. I mean sure, it could totally happen in the timeline, but that kind of erases a lot of the lore established by the entire _Fallout_ story from the first game all the way up to New Vegas. It devalues all the stuff that happened and all the things you did with the characters you play as in those respective games. The Courier walking the Lonesome Road and winning Hoover Dam for the NCR? All that game time only for Shady Sands to get nuked like that? I mean, I suppose it‚Äôs a good way to get the audience to hate Hank MacLean even more, but I personally think it wasn‚Äôt the best choice.  Those things are all forgivable, though. I just keep the scene of the Vault-Tec council out of my head-canon because of these nitpicks, plus I would personally prefer that the origins of the Great War are left unexplained. Some things are better left that way, and in my opinion, who struck first falls under that umbrella.

To be obese like a handful of these vault people they would have to eat 2-5 times more calories per day than other people.  And, the result would be a less healthy person that requires more medical resources throughout their life.   The vaults sealed people away for over 200 years.  They spoke about 80 people in one of the vaults.  They were cautious enough about food consumption that dogs weren't allowed.  They have to be careful about how they use resources.  Yes, they're able to grow some food to supplement.  How many calories are in in the vegetables they're growing? Here's why I find the Fallout series of games share similarities with the Odyssey and the Iliad:  The Fallout series, much like the Odyssey and the Iliad, explores themes of heroism, resilience, and the human experience in the face of adversity. In the Odyssey, we follow the epic journey of Odysseus as he faces numerous challenges and battles on his quest to return home. Similarly, in Fallout, players navigate a post-apocalyptic world filled with dangers, moral dilemmas, and quests for survival.  One key similarity between Fallout and the ancient epics is the concept of a hero's journey. In both the Odyssey and Fallout, the protagonists embark on perilous adventures, encountering allies, enemies, and supernatural forces along the way. These journeys test the characters' courage, wit, and resolve, mirroring the challenges faced by the Vault Dwellers and Lone Wanderers in the Fallout series.  Furthermore, both the Iliad and Fallout explore the consequences of war and conflict. In the Iliad, the Trojan War serves as a backdrop for examining themes of honor, glory, and the devastating impact of battle on individuals and societies. Similarly, Fallout portrays a world ravaged by nuclear war, where survivors must navigate the harsh realities of a post-apocalyptic landscape shaped by violence, power struggles, and the remnants of a once-thriving civilization.  Moreover, the themes of loyalty, betrayal, and the search for identity resonate across all three works. In the Odyssey, Odysseus grapples with his identity and his longing for home, while in Fallout, players often face moral choices that test their allegiances and values. The intricate relationships between characters in the Iliad mirror the alliances and conflicts that players encounter in the Fallout series, highlighting the complexities of human interactions in times of turmoil.  Additionally, both the Odyssey and Fallout incorporate elements of the supernatural. From the intervention of gods and mythical creatures in the ancient epics to mutated creatures and advanced technology in Fallout, these fantastical elements add depth and intrigue to the narratives, blurring the lines between reality and myth.  In conclusion, the Fallout series shares striking similarities with the Odyssey and the Iliad in terms of thematic depth, character development, and exploration of the human experience in extraordinary circumstances. Through their epic storytelling, these works invite audiences to ponder timeless questions about heroism, morality, and the enduring resilience of the human spirit in the face of daunting challenges.

My biggest complaint was how stupid everyone in the show is. Other than that I LOVED it
The show was very entertaining and fun to watch but it has soooo many inconsistencies with the lore, that it's kinda hard to ignore, especially seeing, that I'm a pretty big Fallout nerd and seeing as the show has been declared cannon. I think I'd rather have a worse show, than a good show potentially messing with the quality of the lore in future games and/or even retconning events (or non-events) in previous games. I think this dilemma would've been honestly solved by not making the show take place in California but some place, that was never explored in Fallout. The downfall and destruction of Shady Sands for example is a huge problem for me. In the show it took place before the events of New Vegas, yet in New Vegas, no one even mentions the complete destruction of the NCR's capital. The NCR is still going strong, expanding and fighting multiple wars on multiple fronts, while in the show, the NCR seems to have almost completely vanished in California, which does not make much sense, when looking at Fallout New Vegas. Another example is the Master. He was based in a test vault near the L.A. Boneyard and he sent his Super Mutant troops all over California, in search for "prime normals", as they so lovingly put it. Infecting normal Wastelanders, who had to endure high amounts of radiation, resulted in the big dumb Super Mutants we know and love but infecting people with the FEV, who were not radioactive (prime normals), would result in highly intelligent Super Mutants, who could even develop psychic powers and to achieve that, the Master raided Vaults in California, to kidnap in infect the inhabitants, so they could serve as higher ranking members of his army. I just don't see a possibility, in which vaults 31, 32, 33 and 4 (especially seeing as Vault 4 let in people from the Wastes) could've survived the Master, as they're all on the surface and easy to find and access. The Master went after every single prime normal, he could get his hands on, there's just now way, that these Vaults could have survived. These are just two examples because listing every single one, would honestly take me a while. I can understand people hating this show because the lore is not taken very seriously (just look at what they did to the Brotherhood, they're suddenly actual medieval larpers lol). I have a buddy, who works in the industry and he says, that no one takes the lore seriously at these video game productions, which could explain as to why they often suck. No one cares for the lore but the fans and they want to make money with show, so they take "creative liberties" with the property, to widen mass appeal, which usually pisses off the fans.  The show is entertaining in my opinion, I quite liked it but only because I actively chose to suspend my disbelief, just like you did. Sometimes I had to shake my head in disappointment because they just casually and probably unintentionally retconned something but what can you do? I just tried taking it for what it is and not think about the established lore too much. This is a brain off show. However, fans shouldn't have to actively turn off their brains to enjoy a show and I can understand, if people don't want to do that and hate the show as a consequence. I would hate it too. Ultimately, we'll have to see how it continues. Show's not over yet and maybe they'll make it make sense somehow, even though I doubt it. We'll just have to see how it affects future games or even past games, if things go really bad (for example declaring past games as not canon because of the discrepancies would be the worst possible outcome, even though I am 99% sure, that they'd never do that). Even though I quite enjoyed watching it, I'm still undecided as to what to think of it. It's an entertaining piece of media for sure but if it will be worth it, remains to be seen, at least for me
My only complaint was the nudity just because it's awkward when watching with family but I loved this show

In the fact that in my opinion, the main questline flip flops between mid/decently good. All the while, the sideplots and certain character backstories are what really made the show interesting. i love the show but MAN does it piss me off (spoilers from here on, and yes, despite my bitching to come, i do recommend you watch it if you haven't)    I feel like how the NCR was handled was... crap, and that's my largest complaint. as far as I can tell, it seems as though the house ending is going to be the cannon one for the series so it does kind of make sense that after loosing hoover dam, and then having shady sands glassed that it would collapse. but that fact that it was nuked at all is just... it leaves a bad taste in my mouth.  not only do we not know where the nuke was even launched from, the fact Vault-Tec apparently started the war at all (something i feel like should've eternally been kept ambiguous, and most certainly not explained in a flashback) just feels like an overdone trope that they didn't justify using (huur durr, big corpo will start apocalypse to increase profit and take over the world) and the fact that it seems as though the nuke was perfectly timed with the height of political instability in the NCR to make everything come falling apart just because some random ass overseer from before the war got mad that his wife left him is just... why? (also what the hell happened to the water chip being fucked, they kind of just brought it up then dropped it)  back to the NCR though, I wish it was the followers of the apocalypse at the end. it would be MUCH more in theme for the followers to track down cold fusion (although, i do have problems with it being made before regular fusion* but that's a minor gripe) and use it to power the whole of an area, effectively forever. the way that the fight plays out also leaves a bad taste in my mouth, like are these borderline raiders supposed to be the remnants of the NCR? what happened to the uniforms? the weapons? the organization? the only thing that makes them supposedly related to the NCR is that one guy is running around with a flag. it feels eerily similar to how Ulysses describes how the tribe he trained braided their hair to honor him, not knowing the importance of why he still did his hair that way, but I really feel like its giving the show too much credit to throw in a reference to mr million words himself.  then there's the fact there's absolutely no remaining bits of anything left from the NCR, this was a republic that lasted over 100 years and there's nothing to show, not a spec of old uniforms, weapons, infrastructure. i get people not going "oh remember the NCR" because that'd just be the lead up to an exposition dump but still, there's no evidence that quite recently there was an outright Nation in the area that the story takes place in.   and from now I'm gonna be going on about how I like the way the show develops things, while still critiquing it.  The west coast chapter of the BOS being the first responders to the nuking of shady sands is cool as hell, and makes sense. though, there is an inconsistency if the house ending is cannon where they should be dead (though that might mean that we're looking at the independent New Vegas at the end of the season). while still talking about the BOS, it seems as though the BOS ending in 4 is the cannon one, as the fact the prydwen is in the air and there's a mention of "commonwealth command" (let alone the fact they got the info of an enclave defector/escapee) all but means that the BOS has the commonwealth under its grip (and is possibly stagnating because of it), i find it an interesting choice to even touch on the commonwealth and set anything there in stone, but things can still be done to spice things up further so im not too fussed about that  as for Cooper, I love how they make him so skilled. this is a person who's been around for over 200 years, they've 100% picked up a thing or two (or fifty), and his memory not being 100% (forgetting a pretty major flaw in power armor, before then recalling him grilling a westek suit for getting people killed from that same flaw) and almost getting him killed during his fight with maximus despite him being able to down multiple knights during the raid on the observatory feels like a satisfying explanation, though how they handle ghouls as a whole is kind of annoying. as far as I can tell, its not that every single ghoul needs the magic no feralization drug, but instead it prevents them from becoming completely feral once the process begins. this is the type of thing that I feel like needs an explanation. how did it come to be, why is it that some random ass bums running an organ harvesting operation have so much, why is it that it apparently this drug was manufactured pre-war and widespread to the point of being in pharmacies despite the fact ghoulification as a whole was very poorly understood? I'm fine with going and saying its exclusion from previous games was because it just isn't shown or something like that, because sometimes you have to do that. but the drug itself is where I find issue as its not given a satisfying explanation (I mean hell, having it be a remnant of the NCR's work or even the followers of the apocalypse would be cool, instead its just a McGuffin)  I do like how Lucy walks the balance of a wide-eyed optimist when she feels like she's comfortable (and what horrors she's comfortable with evolving as she spends more time on the surface) before having that repeatedly stomped out, only for its stubborn head to rear again when she gets some more understanding and skill of given situations. she's no genius, but she's a well made resourceful character with some dangerous behavioral habits.  as for how they handled Mr. House, it could've been done a lot better. him attending that meeting and not just being a no-show feels like a copout from his ""mathematical certainty"" line and takes away from the characters intelligence IMO, mainly because I'm sure Vault-Tec would've given him a date as to when shit was gonna go down, and even if they didn't he would be the type to notice a fuck ton of Vault-Tec employees going to 31-33 and know things were going down. yet, he still didn't manage to get the platinum chip in time. if he knew things were going down on that day, then lets be real there would've been a tracker with the courier delivering the chip before the bombs dropped so house would know where it was down to the street. other than House though the rest of the meeting (other than the whole "hurr durr" plot bit I went on about earlier) was sufficiently morbid. because yeah, the owners of these companies are flat out unhinged and we know this quite well by now, I mean fuck we especially know that the things Big MT would do would be seriously fucked considering their track record at the Sierra Madre and likely would be the most sadistic ones we see in the franchise.   and my final gripe is radiation, we see only one radaway if I remember right and occasionally hear that Geiger counter click but it feels like it never has an impact. nobody gets radiation sickness, and it seems there's no dangers from being in highly radioactive areas, but I will give it to the writers that having the raiders be detectable by a Geiger counter feels smart and is a satisfying way for Lucy to find out that somehow people from the surface got inside (a lesser writer would've had it be where she just so happens to wander up to the vault like she always does to remember her mother and mope around about how bummed she is to not be able to leave the vault, when suddenly after going to the door, she finds it open! shock shock horror horror, go down and blah blah blah.)  anyway, that was a thoroughly unproductive hour or so, imma leave it there,  TL:DR I'm mad at the show for the mistakes it makes and the pitfalls it falls into, but still think its damn good and overall is a good adaptation.

One thing I haven't seen anyone mention yet is that the show was not just homages to Fallout lore but also Sergio Leone spaghetti westerns. They clearly make Lucy, the Ghoul, and Maximus into the Good, the Bad, and the Ugly with the Ghoul's theme making reference to Eastwood's theme in the movie. I love how Goggins's character is basically Clint Eastwood, a western star known for his gritty performances who then his gritty bounty hunter character in real life after the bombs drop. They even made a reference to John Wayne in the part where Cooper doesn't want to shoot the bad guy in the head because a good sheriff wouldn't do that, as John Wayne famously said he hated the direction westerns were headed where the main character is an anti-hero instead of a good guy.  The writers just cared and you could see it in every scene and Goggins delivers every line with perfect precision.

It wasn't directly spoken to here, so maybe it was just the chat in another Fallout Critique, but maybe it was passingly ref'd as ..."the things it does wrong," so Maximus. I've seen some speak ill of the character and actor, but I think if you pause to think about what a person would be like after having been so traumatized at a young age and then dropped into the abuses of the Steel Brotherhood. His personality is as expected. The Brotherhood lacks any form of military discipline, esprit de corps, or leadership and is just a mill for sociopaths. Maximus' personality is muted in the extreme and long moments are taken for him to respond to questions as the requirements for organizational zealotry is balanced against a survivor's desire to live. It is well written, on point and because of that the arc of maximus' character is potentially the greatest of all. The interplay between Lucy and Maximus, their budding romance, is the story of the oddest couple. Extremes that compliment and inspire. I could go on, but this makes my point. If someone wants to dare me, I will do a scene-by-scene literary analysis, but be warned, I take no prisoners. ;)

Here's something I don't think very many people have considered. _What if_ the Fallout TV show is doing the _exact_ same thing that the Halo TV show is doing, but they're doing it _right?_  I see a lot of people talking about how this show is retconning things, and saying that Todd Howard saying the show is canon means... Well, they're saying that means a _slew_ of different things, but, two questions. 1) What, exactly, is it retconning? 2) What if he's right?  To clarify 1, the main thing I hear people say that it's retconning is the whole Fall of Shady Sands in 2277 thing. In New Vegas, which _canonically_ takes place in 2081, there is _plenty_ of talk of how the NCR is on the decline. I think even Ulysses has a line in Lonesome Road about the Bear eating itself from within due to the corruption and what-not. _Canonically,_ the Second Battle of Hoover Dam is in 2082, so what if Maximus's flashback takes place in 2283? While, sure, news about it _could have_ travelled to the Commonwealth for Fallout 4 which begins (again) in 2287, what _purpose_ would it serve to inform people who know _diddly dick_ about the NCR and its woes? The Brotherhood had other things to worry about, and that's _assuming_ the chapters were even in contact.  As for question 2, _what if_ the show - which the majority of it takes place in 2297 - is canon to _its own history?_ It uses the games' stories and lore as a springboard, but at _certain points_ there are forks in the timeline where certain events happened, and other events _didn't_ happen? Fallout 5, the game, _could_ come out and take place during the same time period _and_ region and have _none_ of the show's story beats.  I dunno. I enjoyed the show. I thought it was well done, well acted, well written, and well paced. It's a great example of what happens when video game adaptations are done _right._ I'll admit, after what I _heard of_ Halo, I was skeptical, but I'm glad that for once such pause was unwarranted. Bethesda _has_ had a couple misses of late (Redfall and...), _including_ in the Fallout series (... 76, anyone?), but this... This I enjoyed. _As I did_ *parts* of Starfield (now make that pie whole, Todd!). I'm _damn excited_ for Season 2 when it finally comes out. :D

As a gamer and avid fan, I think the Fallout fanbase is divide by two sides of the fandoms: 1. Non Bethesda Fallout games: From what I've seen, the non Bethesda made Fallout games have the theme of society rebuilding after an apocalyptic event while the 50's aesthetics are much weaker. 2. Bethesda Fallout games: Keeping the games in a post apocalyptic world with strong 50's aesthetics down to the fashion, people and themes. This is evident in the show and Fallout 76 as Vault-Tec likes to nuke or destroy any advanced societies (Shady Sands and The Institute). Other argue that Fallout games get stripped of it's RPG elements for a more streamlined action RPG game. 3. This is Bethesda/Todd Howard's more or less first go on the West Coast (Fallout 1, 2 and New Vegas were Interplay and Obsidian games). The show has some BIG fuckups. Not so much in terms of lore reasons, but just in terms of stuff that makes no fucking sense whatsoever and things that back track heavily on some things that had been established long ago in the west coast region.   Firstly, and the most glaring problem, is the who and why behind the nuking of Shady Sands. I won't spoil it here, but it's such a massive let down in the perspective of the rest of the world and all the other shit going on or that has happened in regards to that region and the NCR as a faction. Another massive problem with Shady Sands getting nuked is just another example of Bethesda being allergic to letting civilization develop again in the wasteland which was a theme in Fallout 2 and New Vegas which introduced some of the most interesting plot lines in the entire series. It also makes no attempt to address anything else about the NCR within the show despite the fact that they would have a massive presence that would be impossible to ignore in the region.  Perhaps the largest lore error that no one seems to have pointed out is the fact that the BoS shouldn't even be nearly as powerful as they're shown to be in the west coast as they were nearly annihilated by the NCR that forced them back into hiding. The BoS entire characterization feels so odd compared to how they were portrayed in previous games as well. They felt weirdly incompetent and comically evil. It didn't feel like the BoS that was in the other games.  Something that was not touched on again at all was what the fuck happened in vault 32 that made them all turn on each other and kill each other. It gets all this build up but doesn't get addressed after it gets cleaned up.  Another thing that the show just seemed to forget about is how Moldaver seemed to have this weird cult like following around her by former NCR citizens, when NCR citizens had never been characterized as being that superstitious or believing that an actual woman is this deity figure that they pray to and do rituals for. It uses it for a single gag and doesn't address it at all again. The show also doesn't seem to make up it's mind on if Moldaver is this big scary raider or if she's an NCR holdout trying to help the people of the wasteland.   Finally, it also makes zero sense that all these big businesses all somehow believe it's at all profitable to try and accelerate the end of the world when killing almost everyone would mean there's no one to sell your products to. Killing your customers is a terrible idea, and Mr. House even addresses that in New Vegas but seems to think it's a good idea in the last episode, an outright contradiction in characterization yet again.  Overall, the show is fine and fun and has some really good moments, but I don't like that people are just acting like there AREN'T glaring issues with it just because the show wasn't actually horrible.

Ah yes, the show that retcons New Vegas out of existence by nuking the NCR back to the stone age is both good and consistent.  You know, New Vegas, the objectively best written of the four modern Fallouts. The one that people continue to argue over to this day over who is right and wrong.  I guess that means House won in New Vegas. Except not really, huh? Without the NCR, the followers lose their biggest support in the region. Edward Sallow probably never ends up captured by tribals in arizona, never conquers his tribes, never takes up his mantle as Caesar.
